B003XF1XOQ|4.0|Without spoiling the story, there isn't much to say about this last book.  I know exactly why I zipped through The Hunger Games and Catching Fire-- I am fascinated by survival of the fittest scenarios, and both of the first two books provided that in their own way.  The girlier parts of me appreciated the romantic element in the triology (::cough::teampeeta::cough::), but even if that had not been a factor, I would have enjoyed the books immensely.  Mockingjay did not share that terrifying, car-wreck-you-can't-turn-away-from feeling that drew me into the original story, and therefore, it wasn't as enjoyable a read.<br /><br />[Oh gracious... I should stop to clarify-- I would never condone the Hunger Games, obviously; that's not what I mean at all.  But you are granted a certain freedom in fiction to indulge those grittiest parts of your humanity, or inhumanity, and therein lies my \\"fascination\\".  I would be terrified if any of these events actually evolved into reality.<br /><br />Right, so I'm not a terrible person.  It's important that all the random Amazon review-reading citizens of the internet know that.  Moving along...]<br /><br />Mockingjay was to this series what Return of the Jedi was to Star Wars.  It tied up, or in some cases simply severed, loose ends, it brought what we all knew was coming to fruition, and it did so while introducing a new cast of characters, as had been done in each of the previous books, for us to adore or detest.  I wish there had been more development of some of the key figures in this final book, as many of the most interesting ones don't seem to last very long.  It's difficult to become attached to characters, and thus become emotionally devastated when they are inevitably killed off, when you don't have the opportunity to grow to love them.  Maybe the book needed to be longer, or maybe there needed to be more dialogue.  Maybe Suzanne should write another couple of books (I would never object to this) from the perspective of these characters to provide us with deeper insight.  At any rate, don't expect to find yourself on the emotional rollercoaster you may have experienced with books 1 and 2.<br /><br />That said, I still read the book with incredible curiosity and enthusiasm, if only to determine which of Katniss' love interests wins her fickle heart and how the final blows against the Capitol play out.  Some reviewers for this final installment of the series seem to feel very passionately that it was a waste of their time and money.  I have to disagree, and remind readers that you can't always get what you want... but Ms. Collins gives us what we needed, desperately, and that is, simply, the end of her fabulous tale of societal change.

So I cannot say that I'm entirely objective about this book.<br /><br />That said, it's brilliant. It's both interesting and informative. The principles outlined are actually quite basic, but very important. And for some reason, American parents have either lost these principles or never had them to begin with. For example, I cannot count how many times a child has come to my house for a play date and NOT acknoledged me with a simple greeting. The French would never allow that. and he author has a wonderfully thoughtful explanation as to why teaching children to greet adults is so important.<br /><br />Just like any a a renting book, you should not expect to agree with everything. For example, whereas the author devotes an entire chapter to the importance of French mothers having a job outside the house, I find that to be shortsighted. Stay-at-home mothers can have very, full rich lives without working. It's a matter of balancing personal needs and interests with domestic responsibities. Not hard to do, and the end result can be wonderful.<br /><br />My advice - if you have children then read the book. It's never too late to teach them new things and never too late for you to adopt new parenting tools.

On the other there was a spark of enjoyment missing from beginning to end.<br /><br />The book is cleverly structured to present a story within a story (and stories within that). B006HGXGWU|5.0|The simplicity of \\"Take Shelter,\\" Jeff Nichols' critically acclaimed 2011 art-house film, is its brilliance. From the opening scene, when a man stands alone and studies an approaching storm, there's a deep instinctive chord that rings like the chime of distant sirens. Storms kill, and nature's ominous fury can scatter humans like so many insects. The storm's pall covers the film through a series of dreams, and its producing a dark rain drenching the land in \\"motor oil\\" sludge. On a regular basis, we read about global warming and its effects upon our planet. We watch Werner Herzog's 2007 documentary [[ASIN:B001DWNUDI Encounters at the End of the World [Blu-ray]]], in which a population of isolated scientists working in the Antarctica respond unanimously without hesitation that its affects are increasing on a monthly basis. Hurricanes and tornadoes are more numerous than ever before, slamming New Orleans and Joplin, Missouri. Ancient ice is cracking for the first time in recorded history. Something terrifyingly dangerous is on the horizon and it's marching towards us.<br /><br />The always interesting actor Michael Shannon plays Curtis, a modest Ohio family man who struggles to make ends meet. He wears a hardhat on the job as a contractor, sweating daily with machines that drill into the delicate earth to create trenches. He works alongside his best friend Dewart (Shea Whigham), brown-bagging lunch and having a beer afterwards at the local pub. In the morning, he has a quick breakfast of scrambled eggs with his wife Samantha (Jessica Chastain) and daughter Hannah (Tova Stewart). Their daughter is deaf, and they discuss the difficult finances that might allow her corrective surgery. The vivid dreams continue with the world wracked by storms and an unseen menace attacking his home. We learn mental illness runs in his family and Curtis, in a beautifully subtle scene, pays a visit to his mother (Kathy Baker) who suffers from schizophrenia. Finding no comfortable answers,he checks in with the family doctor and tries to explain his terrifying visions. Curtis cannot overcome a deep, growing dread of approaching danger, and he is soon constructing elaborate additions to the backyard tornado shelter.<br /><br />\\"Take Shelter\\" is one of those rare films where one is never sure what is going to happen next. There is suspense, but the drama's driving force is Curtis' attempts to remain calm when terror is just beneath his panicked surface. Every fiber of his being is telling him to take shelter with his family, and he jeopardizes a good life to do just that. We're never sure if the visions are valid or if Curtis is suffering an extraordinary mental breakdown, but his irrational behavior soon alienates friends and neighbors.<br /><br />There is no convenient category to place \\"Take Shelter\\" into, though one could call it a quiet version of M. Night Shyamalan's underrated 2008 film [[ASIN:B001HOZWUG The Happening]]. This is also a darker version of Phil Alden Robinson's whimsical 1989 work [[ASIN:B001YV504U Field of Dreams [Blu-ray]]], in which a farmer (Kevin Costner) hears voices and risks his family and career to build a baseball field. But Costner's character was hearing a voice of God, while Shannon's Curtis is possessed by hellish visions of approaching doom. There's a small thread connecting these films in that, the undeniable strength of respectful love holds a couple together no matter the chaos. Chastain's Samantha (in an equally strong performance) is horrified by her husband's behavior, but chooses to stand by him because she knows the goodness within. Together they bore a child and life, and she's not going to give it up easily. With growing unease she takes the helm, making plans to overcome his choices that have risked their financial future.<br /><br />\\"Take Shelter\\" is a disturbingly subtle document of modern times, when the fear of environmental catastrophe, financial recession and the destruction of America's working middle class looms over the land. Curtis' difficult attempts to find proper health care and financing for his daughter's surgery is another stressor having great relevance. It's no wonder all of us don't have breakdowns, looking for protective shelter to ride out a tsunami which may end American society as we know it. Curtis is crumbling from the pressure, but Samantha refuses to allow him to succumb, and together they nod in agreement to face the coming storm.<br /><br />With a few thought provoking moments, what Nichols' incredible work has done is provide profound inspiration to every family struggling to survive. \\"Take Shelter\\" is a film for adults wrought with the humble responsibilities of raising a family, who have avoided by necessity the numbing drugs of 24-hour technological consumerism. Lyrical and haunting, it is a classic statement of the 21st century.

B006UG90RM|5.0|Just like \\"Fair Warning\\", I dismissed this record the first time I heard it.  I noticed the compression, the clumsy lyrics that don't always fit together very well, Dave's corny posturing, Eddie's occasional tendency to over-play, Alex's metronome style of drumming and heavy cymbals, the lack of any discernable melodies, etc.  After the 3rd or 4th listen, I realized what I had in my hands, and I completely changed my mind.<br /><br />Listening to Eddie Van Halen play the guitar is serious business.  This guy is out for blood.  He takes no prisoners.  He is letting the world know that \\"Hey people, I'm back and I can still do this better than anyone else.\\".  Like all brilliant technicians, however, Eddie needs someone to bridge the gap between himself and the audience.  Enter Dave-he provides levity, energy, fearlessness, humor, showmanship, and ah...he's never at a loss for words!  He's not perfect, but he knows that and doesn't really care!  Dave IS the perfect foil for an obsessive-compulsive perfectionist like Eddie.  The tension between these two equally strong personalities, and the way the drama unfolds, is what made this band so great in the first place-and the formula still works.  For anyone who came of age in the 1980's, listening to this record is like having sex with an old girlfriend-you may not get along anymore, but there's one thing that you can still do together perfectly!!!  These guys will be locked in a tug-of-war for the rest of their lives, whether they ever make another record or not.  Great stuff.<br /><br />O.K.  Down to business.  The \\"Fair Warning\\" comparison is valid because this record, just like that one, has no obvious pop-friendly hits upon first glance.  It's dense, unrelenting hard rock that only occasionally mocks itself on tunes such as \\"Stay Frosty\\".  Dave's much-derided vocal technique has actually improved over time.  He no longer has that girlish, high-pitched squeal, but you can really hear him going for it in an operatic falsetto on tunes such as \\"You and Your Blues\\" which lyrically sounds an awful lot like him expressing his views on Ed's personal problems.<br /><br />I've read some of the negative reviews of \\"HoneyBabySweetieDoll\\", and I have to say that I love this track.  After the obvious choices \\"Tattoo\\" and \\"You and Your Blues\\", this one is a stand out for the simple fact that it includes some of Eddie's sonic experiments (his take on \\"Revolution Number 9\\" from the White Album perhaps, along with a pinch of Led Zeppelin's \\"Kashmir\\") and if I'm not mistaken, this is the band's first really impressive foray into eastern music.  It breaks new ground, and after I overcame the initial shock, I found myself coming back to this one over and over again.<br /><br />If you were hoping to hear some new-found maturity from these guys, and some parting bits of wisdom before they move on to the next life, I can tell you that there is very little of that here.  The message I got was \\"Hey, we're just as tough now as we were 30 years ago, and in some ways tougher.  We're not ready to check out just yet.\\".  If you were hoping for some light-weight pop rock along the lines of \\"Dance the Night Away\\", I can tell you that they skipped that almost entirely.  \\"Stay Frosty\\" is a nice bit of levity that really rocks, but it's also wicked and satirical, so it doesn't quite fit the 80's radio confection format.<br /><br />I could say more, but basically if you're a fan of these guys, then you have to hear this.  It's astonishing and it does a nice job of harvesting from the past while at the same time breaking new ground.  It's vital.  It's virile.  It's tough.<br /><br />Enjoy!

B004Z4LYU2|5.0|Einstein's theory of Relativity is incomplete, much like Newton's Laws are incomplete. While the theory and laws work well within their set of assumptions, they start to diverge as conditions move outside their envelope of comfort. Newton's laws, while intuitive, have been proven to produce wrong answers at high speed and intense gravity. Einstein's theory shows great correspondence with experiment - up until the user tries to apply it to Black Holes, or correlate it with Quantum Mechanics, also a well regarded theory in it's own right.<br /><br />The Standard Model can be used to investigate some of these areas, but it doesn't forge new ground. It produces answers when initial conditions are customized, but tells us little or nothing about why those initial conditions are what they are.<br /><br />String Theory is supposed to address these problems, but it doesn't. String Theory is really a large number of theories, each with it's own set of initial conditions, leaving us no further along than the Standard Model. Any advances made using String Theory can be made other ways, sometimes with less effort.<br /><br />There are 5 great, unsolved problems in physics today:<br />  1)  Combine general relativity and quantum theory into a single theory that can claim to be the complete theory of nature.<br />  2)  Resolve the problems in the foundations of quantum mechanics, either by making sense of the theory as it stands or by inventing a new theory that does make sense.<br />  3)  Determine whether or not the various particles and forces can be unified in a theory that explains them all as manifestations of a single, fundamental entity.<br />  4)  Explain how the values of the free constants in the standard model of particle physics are chosen in nature.<br />  5)  Explain dark matter and dark energy. Or, if they don't exist, determine how and why gravity is modified on large scales. More generally, explain why the constants of the standard model of cosmology, includingthe dark energy, have the values they do.<br /><br />The author argues that while the people that study string theory are all very intelligent and hard working, there has been little or no progress in physics due to their efforts in the last 25 to 30 years. These string theory proponents are highly confident that they will find the answers they seek (the answers are always just around the corner), and they monopolize most of the talent and money in physics in the failing effort.<br /><br />That is the trouble with physics.<br /><br />The author is very persuasive and provides a lot of data and good arguments for his points in this book, but more importantly, he provides suggestions for avenues of inquiry by the professional physics society, why this is important for lay people who are the readers of this book, and what we lay people can do to help. His suggestions sound reasonable and doable. Some of the problem descriptions he makes are so well stated and universal that they apply to all features of life, not just physics, and the suggestions gain credence from the association.<br /><br />The book is long and some of the arguments are so complete that the reader just wants the author to move on, but deep rooted problems need that kind of exposure to be heard and understood. Skimming, at those points, is recommended; you'll recognize them when you see them.

0307888908|3.0|I always find it interesting when a career book claims to provide \\"new\\" thinking about careers-- and then basically packages information that's been around for awhile.  This book is another in the line of thinking about your career (and yourself) as a business start-up.  By applying the same rules you would apply to starting a business you will be able to control your career.  It's not a bad idea, but the idea has been around since at least since the early 1980s when the book [[ASIN:0201095033 The Inventurers: Excursions In Life And Career Renewal, Third Edition]] was first written. That book talked about always thinking of yourself as self-employed, again, not a bad idea.<br /><br />And, not surprisingly, given that the book is written by one of the founders of LinkedIn, a large focus of the system is on networking and connections.  But networking as a concept isn't new-- there are just new ways to do it through social networking.<br /><br />The information is good and readers will develop good strategies for managing their careers-- but it's just one more approach, not necessarily the only or the best approach. The author doesn't place much credence in systems that tell people to follow their passions-- and he's right, to a point. Practicality often has to enter the process as well.  But plenty of people have done well in their careers by following their passions.  The point is, there is no one right way to develop a career or find a job.  Different paths work for different people. If thinking of yourself as a business start-up appeals to you, then you will probably like this system.

Maybe I'm biased. Probably I've pigeonholed Channing Tatum. Perhaps I was expecting him to bust an ill dance move or join a fight club. Me, I liked this film okay. And for the film's starry-eyed target audience, no amount of dis will sway them. Still, somewhere, even the staff writers at Hallmark are rolling their eyes.<br /><br />In wintry Chicago, Leo (Tatum) and Paige (Rachel McAdams) are a young married couple still very much in love with each other. After all, they wrote their wedding vows on the menus of their favorite restaurant, and that right there probably qualifies what they have as some sort of legendary love affair. But then a runaway truck smashes into their car, and there's Paige catapulting thru the windshield. Leo, who had his seatbelt on, is relatively fine. When Paige awakens from her coma, she discovers that she's suffering from selective amnesia. She can't remember the last five years of her life. She can't imagine being this in demand sculptor and living in the city and married to Leo, this sudden stranger.<br /><br />I'm sure them womenfolk are swooning all over Channing Tatum (who also bares his behind in one scene, so, uh, yay). Tatum is rendered ridiculously sympathetic by his romantic gestures and by virtue of what he's put thru by the amnesiac Paige. Leo remains the sweet devoted spouse, desperately striving to win his estranged wife all over again. But it's tough, really tough. Once a free-spirited creature, Paige now seems like an entirely different person. One of Leo and Paige's mutual friends voices our concern: \\"I'm just worried if she doesn't remember you, how's she supposed to remember she's in love with you?\\" Somewhere, Nicholas Sparks is kicking himself for not thinking up this plot.<br /><br />Not that Paige is purposely mean or thoughtless. She's experiencing her own bits of turmoil. And, me, what I found really interesting are those moments which focus on Paige and the scary stuff she's going thru. Rachel McAdams is quite good at conveying that sense of desolation and confusion and helplessness one must feel when stricken with deep memory loss.<br /><br />From Paige's perspective, it's like this: She's never had a falling out with her rich, conservative parents (Sam Neill and Jessica Lange). She's still in law school and engaged to her old sweetheart Jeremy (Scott Speedman). Today, Paige can't comprehend what would compel her to move to the city or give up law study or not be on speaking terms with her parents. What made her break up with Jeremy? I remember getting mad at myself in the theater for getting suckered in by these concerns. But I found myself wanting to know what made Paige turn her back on her old life five years ago.<br /><br />Watching the love of your life just slip away from you, no matter how hard you try to hold on - what's a more irresistible premise, especially on Valentine's Day? Channing Tatum's got a long road to go before he achieves real actor status. He still tends to be stiff and awkward. Except that, for this film, his awkwardness works in his favor. It makes him even more of an underdog, especially when pitted against the urbane Speedman and those old acting hands Sam Neill and Jesicca Lange. She remarks: \\"I married him. It must've been for some reason.\\" This then leads to probably my favorite sequence, which is their \\"first date.\\" Since I'm a manly man, I won't swoon or have vapors. But I liked that sequence.<br /><br />THE VOW is based on the real-life story of Kim and Krickitt Carpenter. Apparently, Krickitt never did recover from her amnesia, and yet they're happily married with two kids. Not that I'm saying that's what happens in this movie. If you just forget that Sophie Kinsella is a pseudonym for Madeleine Wickham, author of the dreadful THE GATECRASHER, that is.<br /><br />I'VE GOT YOUR NUMBER is the story of Poppy Wyatt, a young physical therapist living in London and engaged to the too-good-to-be-true Magnus Tavish, a genius from a family of geniuses. Poppy and Magnus barely know one another, but he's proposed, wedding arrangements have been made, and in a few short days they'll be walking down the aisle. By chance, though, Poppy acquires the phone of business consultant Sam Roxton's PA (who chucked it in a garbage can after quitting) after her phone is stolen and her ring goes missing. Poppy decides to help Sam out while using the phone for her own purposes, and of course antics ensue. This is a romantic comedy, after all.<br /><br />While she wasn't the brightest bulb in the box, Poppy was an adorable narrator full of problems, concerns, nave thoughts, and a desire to help that is often misguided. On my eGalley, though, the footnotes would appear on random pages at random places, making the experience confusing. I wish that Kindle would do a better job of incorporating footnotes, but I guess that asking anything of Kindle is a hard bargain.<br /><br />This is a longer book, but after you hit the 50% mark or so, the book flies by. It's quick and fun and definitely Kinsella's best book since CONFESSIONS OF A SHOPAHOLIC. Poppy was no Becky Bloomwood, but she was adorable and I was definitely rooting for her. And I loved Sam! He was such a great character in my opinion, but I am notoriously fond of strong business-minded types over action heroes. Magnus was equally smarmy and skeezy, creating the perfect atmosphere for the story. This book was plotted and paced well for the most part. It was charming, quirky, and adorable.<br /><br />VERDICT: A definite Kinsella success, I'VE GOT YOUR NUMBER is cute and romantic while maintaining a wonderful comedic edge. The album, once McCartney let on to the joke years later, became a collectors' item. But the whole thing of McCartney as socialite muzak maker is part of Paul's lore.<br /><br />Once I started listening to \\"Kisses On The Bottom\\" (a line from the opening song \\"I'm Gonna Sit Right Down and Write Myself a Letter\\"), I was wondering if McCartney was serious or playing up his audience for a laugh. He's played around with expectations before (the three albums as The Fireman with producer Youth, his ventures into classical music, movies, etc,), and he's pulled standards out for albums since the beginning of The Beatles. Nor is he the first performer to decide a shot at the great songbook of the 30's, 40's and 50's would be an artistic challenge. Think Linda Ronstadt's Nelson Riddle phase or Rod Stewart's ongoing \\"Great American Songbook\\" series. To McCartney's credit, he uses Diana Krall's ace band and producer Tommy LiPuma, who could do these arrangements in their sleep. Plus, they went to one of the best old studios in the world, Capitol A, where the likes of Nat King Cole and Fran Sinatra fashioned the kind of sound McCartney strives for here.<br /><br />\\"Kisses On The Bottom\\" succeeds for the most part because of the professionalism. Every note is perfect, every brushed drum stroke sounds bristle-soft, McCartney sings in a register that emphasizes every breath. Even McCartney's two ringers, \\"My Valentine\\" and \\"Only Our Hearts\\" hitch right into line like a jigsaw puzzle. It's - to quote U2 - even better than the real thing. Which is another reason why I'm having such a tough time warming up to more than a C Grade for \\"Kisses On The Bottom.\\" When McCartney and John Lennon teamed up to start The Beatles, these were exactly the kind of songs they were writing against. There's a strange sort of irony the man who buried the kind of standardized pop songs in the 60's simply by shaking his mop-top and singing \\"She loves you, yeah yeah yeah\\" is resurrecting the music he helped to sweep aside for a new big thing. Somewhere, Percy Thrillington is smiling.t. B000068FXR|5.0|The wonderful marriage between wry humor and pathos in the literature of O.Henry can also be found in the exquisitely crafted rock'n'roll of Supertramp. After reading the other reviews, it seems I'm not the only one who considers Crisis? What Crisis? to be the most outstanding Supertramp album, even more so than its illustrious predecesor, Crime of the Century. There are no weak links among the 10 tracks, and the band's distinctive Beatles/Beach Boys/Pink Floyd/jazz influences are all here in full force. John Helliwell once stated that Crisis? What Crisis? was the band's attempt at social commentary, but there must be other reasons to explain why it has mostly languished in relative obscurity:<br /><br />1) No hits. \\"Sister Moonshine\\" did receive some radio play, and \\"Ain't nobody but me\\", \\"A Soapbox Opera\\", \\"Another Man's Woman\\", and \\"Two of us\\" have all been performed many times at the band's concerts, but for the most part, this album's songs just have not been heard by the average \\"classic rock\\" listener.<br /><br />2) Comparisons to Crime of the Century. To this day, Crime is considered by many to be the band's magnum opus. Crisis? What Crisis? was produced under great pressure a little over a year later, and is a very different album. Crime was much darker and featured an elaborate concept (see my review), while Crisis is a lighter, more down-to-earth album. The cover concept (sunbathing gent with golden umbrella and radio oblivious to the dreariness around him) brings home the escapism element. The original inner jacket and sleeve were both painted an unblemished sunny yellow, as if to highlight the bright musical euphoria contained within. Crisis features much more acoustic guitar, and its more subdued moments display an innocence and simplicity that Crime only hinted at. Some reviewers have accused Crisis of duplicating some of Crime's musical textures, with \\"Ain't nobody but me\\" being this album's \\"Bloody Well Right\\", \\"Lady\\", the new \\"Dreamer\\", and the long instrumental close of \\"Another Man's Woman\\" reminding some of the center section of \\"Rudy\\". These can be explained by the chronological proximity of the two albums, and the consistency of the band's signature sound.<br /><br />3) The \\"throwback\\" ethos. The timelessness of Supertramp's music comes to the fore with this album. This is their last \\"English\\" album, as the band permanently relocated to the United States during its recording. \\"Poor Boy\\", a pub-rock shuffle, has a wonderful clarinet solo that's straight out of Benny Goodman or Pete Fountain. \\"A Soapbox Opera\\" (the album's high-concept piece, which some have interpreted as a critique of institutional Catholicism) has an uncreditted female vocalist singing the hymn \\"All Things Bright and Beautiful\\" between the second and third verses. Others have commented on the Copland-esque Americana of \\"Easy Does It\\" and \\"Sister Moonshine\\", the scat vocals at the end of \\"Lady\\", and the Hollywood soundtrack strings on \\"Just a Normal Day\\". While maintaining a contemporary sound throughout, many of this album's songs also have a \\"long ago and far away\\" feel to them.<br /><br />4) The mysterious ending. While Crime of the Century closed with the grand catharsis of its title track, Crisis? What Crisis? reaches its conclusion with two songs that have alienated many. For those who love it, \\"The Meaning\\" (never performed live) is hypnotic, and for those who don't, it's repetitive. Helliwell's soaring soprano sax leads the listener into the setting sun during a very long fadeout. After an extended silence, \\"Two of us\\" serves as the album's peaceful postscript. Rockers may be offended by this ethereal, gather-round-the-harmonium hymn, but it's hard to conceive of any other way this album could have signed off with.<br /><br />This may come as a surprise, but if you enjoy the Breakfast in America and Famous Last Words albums, you should have no trouble with Crisis? What Crisis?, even if you are not familiar with any of its songs.Admittedly, the songs are structured differently on the two later albums, but the classic Supertramp sound is present on all three. That different themes can be cut from the same musical cloth has never been so aptly demonstrated as it has with this band. Drummer Bob Siebenberg has never had a better album, and Roger Hodgson's most vicious guitar solo until \\"Waiting so long\\" appears on \\"Ain't nobody but me\\". For those willing to venture beyond the search for \\"hits\\", Crisis? What Crisis? repays in spades. Thank God quality is not intrinsically linked to popularity.

B00005JLWN|2.0|A conceptual Christian Bale sci-fi vehicle that can't quite commit to its one primary plot point. It's the future, and we're living in a totalitarian regime thanks to a government-mandated drug that saps humanity of its ability to feel. Bale plays the cold, emotionless head of the gestapo, responsible for tracking and exterminating the few remaining shreds of liberal thought in the wild. Curiously, after incinerating every shred of artwork, poetry and literature he can find, this super-cop then retires to his ornately decorated office where a big show is made of the beautiful architecture and lavish sculptural decorations. The story's failure to comply with its own focal point doesn't end there, as Bale's coworkers and bosses are surprisingly animated and emotive for men supposedly removed from their own feelings. The plot offers no revelations, with the protagonist stomping through a shallow character arc and an overly simple, black-and-white evaluation of the grand scheme. B000005GUL|5.0|I compare these two recordings for several main reasons: they usually head the field in recommendations for the best version to buy; they were the first two I heard, almost simultaneously, when as an teenager I was first becoming enraptured by opera; lastly, I happen to own and play both  and listen to them with pretty much equal enjoyment. Neither is perfect but they certainly do justice to this wonderful period-piece which made the sadly short-lived Weber famous throughout Europe.<br /><br />I first heard the Wolf's Glen scene in an EMI sampler LP of operatic highlights and still feel that it is the most atmospheric ever recorded - better than the Kleiber version which, although still effective, doesn't quite capture the Gothic chill and has a rather ordinary-voiced Samiel compared with the hard, abrasive Fritz Hoppe for Keilberth. Where Kleiber particularly scores is in the crowd scenes with the dances and choruses. It was a on a remainder bin LP - only the first LP of the set - that I heard his overture and the opening scene with its natural-sounding crowd noises, mocking laughter and galumphing peasants struck me as enormously invigorating music-making; Kleiber takes the whole thing at a clip and really injects life into proceedings. Keilberth is steadier and more staid, with the grander-sounding BPO but his is also an affectionate and beautifully played account.<br /><br />I give Kleiber five stars even though I readily admit an antipathy to Peter Schreier's throaty, nasal tenor. I really cannot comprehend how some find his pinched voice beautiful but he is impassioned and characterful here, sounding really desperate as Max, so I let it pass. Schock has a more conventionally baritonal heroic tenor and sings agreeably if a tad blandly. Dramatic verisimilitude is enhanced by the fact that Keilberth's singers deliver their own dialogue whereas for Kleiber a very truncated script is employed and actors are used, with the usual mismatch between singing and speaking voices. That abridgement also means that some famous lines are cut, such as that quoted by Bismarck in the Reichstag in 1849: \\"Glaubst du, dieser Adler sei dir geschenkt?\\" (\\"Do you think you get this eagle for nothing?\\").<br /><br />Regarding the leading ladies in both versions, all are superb. There is little to choose between two of the loveliest German post-war sopranos in Elisabeth Gr&uuml;mmer and Gundula Janowitz, both of whom were lyric sopranos who could sing the lighter Wagner roles and to whose silvery voices I could listen all night. Both Lisa Otto and Edith Mathis are delightful as &Auml;nnchen. I prefer Karl Christian Kohn's mostly focused, black-voiced Kaspar for Keilberth to Theo Adam (sometimes unkindly known by his non-admirers as \\"Mr Wobble\\", but passable for Kleiber). Both hermits are impressive, even if a younger-voiced Franz Crass is steadier than the veteran Gottlob Frick - but both bring the requisite kindly gravitas to the role. The Leipzig chorus is livelier and interpretatively freer than the more \\"operatic\\" Berliners; the latter are less animated in their hailing of Kilian and barracking of Max but only comparatively speaking and they respond credibly in little incidents such as when Max draws a knife on Kilian. Both conductors relish the Bauer element in the music. There are a few clumsy tape edits on the EMI recording and its 1958 stereo sound, although fine, is less immediate than the fuller but slightly toppy DG recording from 1973.<br /><br />DG persists in gracing the Originals cover with what is a basic solecism: a double-barrelled shotgun rather than a marksman's rifle. I cannot choose between these two admirable versions but can endorse both as excellent, faithful renditions which do honour to a seminal German opera.voices. But I was always an expatriate.\\"<br /><br />So Cain summarizes her experience as an introvert among extroverts, and likely the experience of many of the hundred-million-plus American introverts -- a third to a half of the US population -- whom extroverts deem to have \\"a second-class personality trait, somewhere between a disappointment and a pathology.\\"<br /><br />Her book is part history, documenting America's move from the farm to the city and from an introverted \\"culture of character,\\" intelligence and creativity to an extroverted \\"culture of personality.\\" It's part biology and psychology, exploring genes and culture/environment toward a thesis that the amygdalas of introverts' brains make them threat-avoidant while extroverts' make them reward-seeking (\\"Introverts like people they meet in friendly contexts; extroverts prefer those they compete with\\"). And it's part how-to, with tactics to better involve introverts in the workplace, tips for parents raising introverts, and rehab (my word) for \\"expatriated\\" adults (\\"[T]hink back to what you loved to do when you were a child [...] pay attention to the work you gravitate to [...and] what you envy\\").<br /><br />Written accessibly and for a wide audience, the book will be revelatory to extroverts who are confounded by quiet people. And for quiet people (like me, also formerly a workplace expat), the book feels like coming home.

B000002UAR|5.0|It's really quite a futile task.  What, you say?  To try and come up with your favorite album ever.  Unless you don't really obsess over music like I do, then maybe it's not so hard.  That Santana album with that dude from the crappy radio band has the most plays on your iTunes player, so that must be it.  Or that one Hootie and the Blowfish album with the song you played when you proposed to your wife back in the summer of '94.  Maybe that's your favorite.  I wish it was that simple for me.  It's not.  I feel like Rain Man around a tub filling with water when asked about my all-time favorite record.<br /><br />In all honesty, picking an artist to sit on the throne of all time favorite  records wasn't that hard.  I knew it would be the Beatles.  I mean, of all the amazing bands I've loved over the years, none have had the impact on me that the Beatles have had.  From the very early years of my life they've been the soundtrack that's played in my head.  I'd hear `Bungalow Bill' on repeat between my ears at school as the teacher would be talking about Indiana History.  As I'd try to fall asleep at night I'd hear `Being for the Benefit of Mr. Kite' echoing in my head and I'd have the craziest visions.  When I was down I'd play `In My Life' and its' melancholy longing for a loved one would somehow bring me out of my funk.  'Across the Universe' would let me disappear into it's surreal landscape and envelope me.  'Getting Better' made me see Paul McCartney not as the lovelorn teen heartthrob, but as possibly some ruffian with several broken hearts under his belt.  'I Should Have Known Better', with Lennon's ever present scratched up and imperfect voice, lamenting about a girl that broke his heart( and knowing from the start she would).  There's the obvious choices: `I Want To Hold Your Hand', `Yesterday', `Twist and Shout', `I'm a Loser', `No Reply', `Drive My Car', `Strawberry Fields Forever', `Taxman', `Got To Get You Into My Life', `Let It Be', `Hey Jude', `A Day In The Life', `With A Little Help From My Friends', `Glass Onion'......well, you get the drift.  You see, pretty much any Beatles record could sit on top of King Music Mountain.  For the sake of argument, and for me to learn to make a damn choice, I've chosen Revolver.<br /><br />Listen, I can't write anything that hasn't already been written, and written far better, with more flowery language and fancier and heartfelt prose than what's already out there regarding the Fab Four.  So quite simply, this is why I've chosen Revolver over The White Album, Sgt. Pepper, Rubber Soul, Beatles For Sale or Abbey Road:  They found their edge.  They got gritty and found an  attitude on this record.  Starting an album out with a track like `Taxman'?  There was a jagged point at the end of that riff.  It wasn't meant to scare, it was meant to hurt.  George Harrison finally got the respect he deserved on a Beatles record.  He got the opening spot and this song is one of their absolute best.  The songs on this record are very distinct to one another.  'Eleanor Rigby', `Dr. Roberts', `Here, There, and Everywhere', `I'm Only Sleeping'.  Each one no longer had numbers in front of them indicating the chronological order on the vinyl.  They had lines drawn in the sand separating them.  Lennon and McCartney had gone from being a musical partnership to an all out musical competition.  And with each song they wrote they were daring the other to top it.  I'm sure there was competition between these two before Revolver.  But it wasn't nearly as prominent and obvious as it was once this album hit listeners ears.  I think this musical one-upping is what made this record stand out so much.  It was also the last great rock album before the highly ornamented(and timeless) Sgt. Pepper came out.  In my mind, I feel this was the end of that rock band that could wipe the stage up with any other band out there, and the more experimental, \\"we can do what we want cause we're the Beatles\\" band were born.  I'mnot complaining.  I love both.  But to know these guys could set up in some small club and bang out `Dr. Roberts' or `She Said, She Said' in front of 50 people and make that crowds faces melt, it gives me a warm feeling inside.  Though by this time it was more like 50, 000 people.  But still.<br /><br />Another striking thing about this record is that their tight live sound was finally captured on this record.  Thanks to George Martin, all their records sounded great.  But there was always this feeling that we weren't hearing that great live band in the studio.  Maybe it was too perfect?  I don't know.  With Revolver I think the essence of the Beatles live sound was captured.  The drums and bass were tight.  The guitars were lean and crisp.  The vocals were right where they needed to be; front and center, but never overpowering the music.  The bass on this record is astounding.  Every nuance, every flowing melody line.  Paul shined on this album.  And without his bass line where would Lennon's experimental, one chord mini psychedelic masterpiece `Tomorrow Never Knows' be?  It wouldn't be nearly as epic.<br /><br />I've gone on long enough about this.  You know the deal.  The Beatles changed popular music history.  The Beethovens of pop music.  I pulled a name out of the Beatles hat and Revolver was chosen.<br /><br />Now go put on `And Your Bird Can Sing' and sing till your voice is gone.  Then sing some more.not complaining.  I love both.  But to know these guys could set up in some small club and bang out `Dr. Roberts' or `She Said, She Said' in front of 50 people and make that crowds faces melt, it gives me a warm feeling inside.  Though by this time it was more like 50, 000 people.  But still.<br /><br />Another striking thing about this record is that their tight live sound was finally captured on this record.  Thanks to George Martin, all their records sounded great.  But there was always this feeling that we weren't hearing that great live band in the studio.  Maybe it was too perfect?  I don't know.  With Revolver I think the essence of the Beatles live sound was captured.  The drums and bass were tight.  The guitars were lean and crisp.  The vocals were right where they needed to be; front and center, but never overpowering the music.  The bass on this record is astounding.  Every nuance, every flowing melody line.  Paul shined on this album.  And without his bass line where would Lennon's experimental, one chord mini psychedelic masterpiece `Tomorrow Never Knows' be?  It wouldn't be nearly as epic.<br /><br />I've gone on long enough about this.  You know the deal.  The Beatles changed popular music history.  The Beethovens of pop music.  I pulled a name out of the Beatles hat and Revolver was chosen.<br /><br />Now go put on `And Your Bird Can Sing' and sing till your voice is gone.  Then sing some more.

B006OFN0SE|5.0|Clint Eastwood is probably a better director than actor, and this movie proves it. J. Edgar is a masterful rendering of the life of J. Edgar Hoover, the first director of the FBI, a political figure far removed from today's public's memory, and we're all the better for it. For those who are not familiar with the person, J. Edgar Hoover was a vain, vile, heartless, media-mongering mama's boy, a paranoid control-freak who had no regard for the Bill of Rights or to anyone he perceived to be a \\"danger.\\" He had the appearance of a bulldog, but as Groucho Marx would say, that would be insulting to bulldogs. He exerted his influence by digging up dirt about public figures, all in the name of national security. Yes, he had the goods on everyone in those files of his. He wreaked much damage to the constitutional fabric of this country, and would order a wire tap at a moment's notice (sound familiar?)on anyone he perceived to be a threat and, as put more fittingly by the Clyde Tolson figure in this movie, someone whose memory will be reviled by history.<br /><br />All these adjectives accurately describe Hoover the man and are brilliantly and brilliantly depicted in this film. For those of us who did not have the honor of living while J.Edgar was with us, probably the most titillating subject the film could portray would be the issue about his sexuality. Eastwood could have covered the subject in several ways, either by showing Hoover as (1) cross-dresser, as some maintain; (2) a closet case; or (3) an indignant clod incapable of having any genuine affection, of whatever variety. Eastwood handled the subject with understatement, but all these possibilities are alluded to, but understated. A more flagrant portrail would have diminished the legitimacy of this moviue, and Eastwood wisely decided for understatement. In an interesting twist, he makes Hoover the victim of Tolson's own aggression, ironic for a person who had so little regard for anyone or any thing which did not conform to his own standards, whatever in heaven's name that might jhave been.<br /><br />Eastwood is underrated as a director. Think of Invictus and Gran Torino. His use of flashbacks and flash forwards didn't work in Flags of Our Fathers. In J.Edgar Eastwood he successfully makes use of these devises, and the flashbacks and flash forwards aid in showing Hoover's fragmented, messed up personality. DeCaprio's depiction of this hated person is equally masterful. This is the first movie in modern memory where he melds into Hoover's character and the viewer can forget that this is Leonardo DiCaprio on the screen playing a movie character, a satsifying and good piece of acting.<br /><br />Eastwood again wisely is not judgmental in his depiction. The events of Hoover's life are shown pretty much as they unfolded. It effectively showed how Hoover blackmailed politicians from whom he sought favor. For example, by that time Hoover sought to stay in his position as director during Kennedy's administration. He did so by requesting Robert Kennedy inform the President that he had reports of JFK's affairs and that they could be \\"damaging\\" to the President's reputation.<br /><br />For non-Baby Boomers, Eastwood's movie should be watched to get an idea of what life was like before Bush and Cheney. For the Baby Boom generation this movie is old news, and reminds us all what a self-righteous hypocrite the man really was in life. Perhaps the real message of this movie is not very much has changed.form to his own standards, whatever in heaven's name that might jhave been.<br /><br />Eastwood is underrated as a director. Most people would rather worship the sterile conformity that feeds our consumeristic nation than step outside the feeding frenzy and savor a new and startling flavor. I wonder how art will continue to survive in a world where its occupants are so tragically losing their ability to see and hear. In an artistic sense it is like Puritanism come full circle, where a creative act of staggering originality incites a witch hunt.<br /><br />\\"The Tree of Life\\" is \\"The Rite of Spring\\" of cinema. 0765356139|2.0|I was going to give this book 3 stars, if solely for the last 100 or so pages. I honestly checked to see if the higher rated reviews was about the same novel I was reading, so I kept on going, if only to see this great twist ending I kept hearing about. But then I decided, you know what, no, I'm giving it 2 stars. The reason I decided to dock it so low was because after the first one succeeded in having me riveted, this dragged me through a banal 600 pages before I actually cared. And not in a sophomore slump kind a way, more like a, creatively uninspired kind of way. I can tell you now, the ONLY reason why I stuck this one out is simply because I bought the trilogy boxset and felt like I would have wasted my money if I didn't read it. The unfortunate trade off came when I realized I had wasted weeks of my time instead of my money. If it wasn't for that reason alone, I would have put this book at the bottom of my to read pile and not cared to finish the trilogy. Getting through this book was a chore. In my humble and honest opinion, this shouldn't have even been a novel, or a trilogy for that matter. The first Mistborn just should have been a stand alone novel, such as Elantris.<br /><br />The biggest reason I can think to give this book 2 stars is that it just didn't know what it wanted to be. It split itself into thirds; a political societal warfare novel, a campy teen romance novel, and a save the world from the fallen Gods/mist novel, and it did none of them well. The latter by the way, literally doesn't even become relevant until well after half way through the book. You'll spend much of your time wondering why this is even called the Well of Ascension. I'll break things down:<br /><br />1.Plot- I love political conflict in fantasy stories, but this missed the mark. Elend Venture is the new king of Luthadel, and outside his walls sits two armies, one being his father Straff, and the other being Cett, a king from an opposing dominance. It begins with a great fight scene involving Vin intercepting Cett's assassins, but.... That's about as much action as you will see for a VERY long time. Fine, I don't need action to be entertained, but it is the way things play out that leaves much to be desired. This is largely what this book is about, and it falls into the biggest flaw Sanderson has with this trilogy; characters sitting in a room speculating about what to do with other characters. If this was meant to build 'that impending doom' like I've heard others say, it just doesn't come off that way. And Elend is such a boyscout of a 'good man' (by the way, Vin calls him that at least 100 times) that I found myself actually disliking his character. No one is that whole heartily 'good'. Unfortunately, speculation is what most of this book is. Speculation, no development, no action, no consequence. It is simply boring.<br /><br />2. The characters- I gave the first book a pass on lax character development simply because I knew this was a trilogy, and that there was room for growth. And in all honesty, Kelsier was awesome enough to make up for more than all of his unremarkable crew members, including Vin. If anything, Sanderson made Kelsier too likable, seeing as how he's only around for one book. I think even Sanderson realized this, because almost all of the main characters resent and even demonize Kelsier throughout much of this book. As if that would negate just how awesome of a character he really was. I didn't buy it, and I REALLY missed Kelsier in this one. I was praying that he would spring back from the dead and take over as a main protagonist. I didn't realize how badly this novel would suffer without him until I spent a couple hundred pages having Elend as his replacement. What the heck happened to his character? What happened to the charm and quick witted remarks he had in the first novel? He just isn't interesting enough. And Vin is no better. She is so insecure in this novel it hurts. When she's not flying through the mists, she's worrying about Elend and if she loves him or if he loves her. She is so whiny and frantically over emotional that it comes off as unbelievable for her to be instinctively right on so many things in the novel. NONE of the characters, save for Sazed and the newcomer Mistborn Zane, are interesting enough to hold their own. And the villains Straff and Cett are so one dimensional that they are borderline cartoonish. The only relationship I found enjoyable was Vin's ever changing relationship with her kandra OreSeur. Oh and the Terriswoman Tindwyl was a nice addition, if only for her ability to berate Elend, which I thoroughly enjoyed.<br /><br />3. Redundancy- Lastly, but certainly not least, is the fact that Sanderson will repeat himself, over, and over, and over, and over again. This is a glaring and infuriating flaw that forced me to skip as many as 10 pages into the book at any given time and still be on board with what was happening. Things that are explicitly said in the narrative are then repeated in the dialogue regular and often. Not the least of which is the fact that we are reminded that Vin was beaten, has an ear ring from her mother, and had an abusive brother, at least once every viewpoint turn. It is almost an insult to the intelligence how much he repeats how allomancy, feruchemy, and other magic works. By this point, its water under the bridge. It would be nice to let our hands go. There are times when the flow of action stops cold in its tracks simply to explain how the magic works. It reads more like Mistborn for Dummies, rather than a Mistborn novel at times. If you think that I'am exaggerating, highlight how many times Vin slayed the Lord Ruler, or Vin was a street urchin, or 'they will all abandon me' thought phrase, and you will lose count like I did. This is the reason why this book is too long.<br /><br />I will have to finish the third book, if only to get my money's worth.

B006UG90RM|5.0|Alright, I bought the album, I've listened to it repeatedly, and have, for curiosity's sake, read numerous reviews of the album here on Amazon.com.<br /><br />Let me pre-empt those who will say of my review, \\"well, he's just an old guy from the 80's generation that's trying to relive memories\\" or \\"he's one of those VH fans who will love anything Roth does with the band for old times' sake.\\"<br /><br />Indeed, I do admire VH, especially the DLR era albums 79-84 but was never a huge fan back in the proverbial day. I've actually come to like them more as I've gotten older and become completely disheartened by the lack of talent in the modern music scene. Heck, even the bands I hated in the 70's and 80's are starting to look like Mozart when compared to most of the s**t that's been thrown at us by the current music culture. Call me a dinosaur if you will. I've been playing guitar since I was in 5th grade (now I'm 45...do the math) and have been teaching the instrument since I was in college. I feel fairly confident that I can tell a decent album when I hear it.<br /><br />I'm not a bandwagon type of guy and thus I hope you will take me to task when I encourage you to get this album if you like hard rock music.<br /><br />Eddie simply blew me away with his playing on this album. My rankings for all the VH albums in order based on quality would be: Women and Children First, VH2, VH1, A Different Kind of Truth, Fair Warning, Diver Down, 1984 then yadda yadda yadda (all the pop baloney with Sammy). I never listened to the Cherone album so I can't give that one a rating. Thus, in my opinion, this is the 4th best VH album of all time.<br /><br />I did not pre-order this album. I had no presumptions about it. Though I clearly enjoy the DLR albums more than the Sammy stuff, I was not prepared to give it a positive or negative rating one way or the other before I thoroughly listened to it. Was I hopeful that it would blow me away? Of course, I was. Did I have an axe to grind (pardon the pun)? Not at all. I'm actually a bigger fan of players like Michael Schenker, Gary Moore and Ritchie Blackmore than I am of Van Halen; however, I've always admired Eddie's unique playing style.<br /><br />All that said, get this album. It's worth every penny IMO. Certainly, there are some shortcomings as the nay-sayers have exhorted. David Lee Roth's voice is not the same as it was in the 70's or 80's but give the guy a break. He's old enough to be the grandpa of most of today's music audience! Can your grandpa sing like he can? I highly doubt it. All the older rock singers have lost a step or two; it's part of growing older. I liken DLR's performance on this album to some of the Robert Plant solo stuff. Once Plant got a little older he lost some of his range but wisely adapted his style to one that suited his age. He no longer wails like he did on Whole Lotta Love but he can still lay down some impressive vocals. Roth's performance here is clearly not of the same ilk as the growls and screams from VH1 or VH2 but he has adapted and his vocals are still powerful, in a more refined way perhaps.<br /><br />There's a chemistry with some bands that you can't ignore even when the principal members may hate each other through and through. Dokken and UFO come to mind. Don Dokken and George Lynch clearly dislike each other as do Michael Schenker and Phil Mogg. However, when these groups make music together with their traditional line-ups, there's generally some sort of magic that happens. Van Halen with DLR is like that. I'm not a Sammy Hagar hater. Far from it. In fact, I was into Sammy's solo stuff more than I was into VH in the early 80's. I tried to listen to 5150 the other day after listening to the new VH album and I had to stop it halfway through. There's a sickening poppy-ooze that made my stomach turn.<br /><br />Conversely, the new VH album will totally rock you. Though there may be some compression of the dynamic range, I think it still kicks you in the teeth if you play it through a REAL stereo (not ear buds).<br /><br />If it means anything to hear an honest review from someone who's not a die-hard VH fan then my work here has been worthwhile. I think this album has the potential to show some of the young pups out there that want to be guitar players, what a real guitar album truly is. I hope this album drives young musicians to once again strive for technical precision and for mastery of their instrument.<br /><br />I'm always saddened by the apathy that has come out the 90's and 00's. In our instant-gratification society, people don't seem to want to work hard to achieve something worthwhile anymore. Kids WANT to be great guitarists but they don't really want to work at it the way players like Eddie Van Halen, Michael Schenker and Gary Moore (God rest his soul) had to. To quote some Greek philosophy, \\"Nothing great was ever accomplished simply by wishing for it.\\" I'm a school teacher as well, and can sadly attest that this is true of the attitude towards schoolwork today. Pardon my stance on the soapbox if you will and GO GET THIS ALBUM!<br /><br />By the way, my 3 year old and 6 year old sons want it to be stated that \\"Bullethead\\" is their favorite song on the new VH album! I'm partial to \\"Honey Baby Sweetie Doll.\\"<br /><br />God Bless and Keep Rocking!eeth if you play it through a REAL stereo (not ear buds).<br /><br />If it means anything to hear an honest review from someone who's not a die-hard VH fan then my work here has been worthwhile. I think this album has the potential to show some of the young pups out there that want to be guitar players, what a real guitar album truly is. I hope this album drives young musicians to once again strive for technical precision and for mastery of their instrument.<br /><br />I'm always saddened by the apathy that has come out the 90's and 00's. Kids WANT to be great guitarists but they don't really want to work at it the way players like Eddie Van Halen, Michael Schenker and Gary Moore (God rest his soul) had to. Not coincidentally, this book was released about the same time.<br /><br />This book angers many writers by clearly advising they write first for a chosen niche of readers. This is marketing 101, and good for nonfiction, but novels . . . ?<br /><br />Traditionally, novelists have written to please themselves, perhaps making changing to please editors and genre standards, but still transmitting their own personal vision. If what you happen to write resonates with millions of readers, you're a bestseller. If what you happen to write resonates with very few, you remain obscure.<br /><br />It's partially about genre, but not entirely. Less than 40 years after horror pioneer HP Lovecraft died penniless, Stephen King began making a fortune from his horror novels. The market greatly changed and expanded. But it's also true King writes about ordinary people in unordinary circumstances, not the weirdos Lovecraft did.<br /><br />Yet Locke advises picking a niche, studying those readers, and writing books that appeal to them rather than to you.<br /><br />I believe that's worthwhile advice, especially for writers who, spoiled by their success, have forgotten what appeals to their usual readers.

In the later stages of the game your station is literally encased in a spiderweb of connected constructs. The playing field is zoomable, allowing you to isolate specific areas of your defenses or pull out to see the entire field.<br /><br />The game provides a campaign and three genre-standard \\"endless\\" modes, the endless modes each having a different strategy (survival, mining and protect). Unlike many similar games, the endless modes in this game are NOT simply to entertain you after you've completed the campaign mode, but rather are necessary to accumulate enough credits to buy the units/upgrades necessary to complete the campaign missions. Because of this, I think that I have played and enjoyed the \\"endless\\" modes more than any other such game.<br /><br />The game operations function flawlessly, it is EXTREMELY conservative with battery power, there is a quit button in the main menu and OpenFeint, while included, is unobtrusive and activated only if desired. The only complaint I would have is that due to the sketchy nature of the fire touchscreen and the icon size of the defensive emplacements it can be a bit tricky on occasion to select the  unit you are trying to upgrade. A stylus pretty much eliminates that problem, and again I put this down to the responsiveness of the Fire rather than an issue with the game itself.<br /><br />If you haven't snapped up this game at $.99 yet, do so. If not, the $1.95 list price is still a bargain for the hours of playablity this game provides.

I must say, I ran into some of the exact some issues that others have mentioned. In the end, with a little patience, research, and luck, I was able to get the unit to work. Here is the process I took.<br /><br />First off, when I plugged the wireless adapter into the NR509 (I followed the manuals instructions through this whole process), I decided to go with a manual connection. The receiver allows you to choose between a manual connection (basically entering in your router's info and password after the receiver locates the router) or an automatic connection (entering a PIN code or pressing a WSP button to initialize connection through the router). When I began the manual connection, multiple wireless routers that were present appeared, including mine. I selected my wireless router (it's a Linksy's E1200 with WPA2) and entered in my password. I entered my password in incorrectly the first time, then correctly the second time but still had no connection. This is when things got interesting. From this point on, the receiver no longer showed any present wireless routers, including mine. I then looked into using an automatic connection. Well, the receiver was supposed to display an eight-digit PIN code, but it either displayed no code or simply an \\"@\\" symbol, which did not work. I also tried the WSP button to no avail. After reading about this problem online (I'm not alone), I decided to run a factory reset (see Onkyo online about this) and start over. I would be careful about being rough with the receiver just because the wireless connection is acting up. I wasgoing to call Onkyo support if it wouldn't connect after one factory reset. After the factory reset, the receiver picked up all the original wireless routers; however, I ran into the same manual connection problems as before. I decided to try the automatic-connection option again. This time, the automatic-connection, eight-digit PIN code was present. In order to use this, you have to connect to your router via the advanced options and manage your router through your web browser. I entered the code in my router's correct advanced options field, but the connection failed once again. I also ran the WSP button in the advanced options again, and this also failed connection again. After trying each scenario twice, I still could not get a connection. After working on this for a couple hours, I was about to call it quits, get some sleep (the small UWF-1 adapter probably would have haunted me in my sleep), and call support the next day. However, I ran the automatic, WSP-button connection option one more time (you have to go to the advanced router options in your browser, and this automatic-connection option and the automatic PIN-code option should be listed under the \\"Wireless\\" menu). To my surprise, it actually worked. I ran the firmware update for the receiver, and I have not had any connection problems since (of course, it has only been one day, so if I have any issues, I will update this review). My wireless connection has a strength between 50% and 80% and continues to work the next time I boot up the receiver even if I unplug the router or turn off the receiver. I have since been able to listen to streaming internet with Vtuner. I would say, if you think you can get this to work, it is worth it. The Vtuner (included membership with my receiver) option alone is worth it, as it allows you to choose between dozens of genres of music or talk radio, from dozens of stations, in dozens of countries from around the world.<br /><br />Also, I should say that my receiver with UWF-1 wireless adapter is only about 10 feet away from my wireless router, so i can't speak for how far the wireless signal can go. Considering how small this wireless adapter is, I wouldn't be surprised if it does not have great distance signal strength (of course, I have no idea if wireless adapter size correlates to distance signal strength).<br /><br />In the end, if you have a little patience, do a little research, and if, IF, lady luck is on your side, this wireless adapter can work just fine. I am not a computer expert by any stretch (probably an average computer know-how), and I was able to meander my way through the maze to get this to work. If you take the time, and can get this to work, it is worth the purchase.

B003Y5H5HO|3.0|The major difference between the Swedish version and the American version, is the portrayal of Lisbeth Salander.<br /><br />First: If you read the novels, you know that Lisbeth Salander was only 5 feet tall and weighed 90 lbs.  In the Swedish original, Noomi Rapace is actually 5'3\\" tall, and in the American remake Rooney Mara is 5'6\\".  Folks, there was a reason author Stieg Larsson made his heroine 5 feet tall.  He wanted this little girl to be a gallon of nitroglycerine read to explode -- like she did when she kicked ass on two Hells Angel types.  This was an essential part of the plot -- that someone so small, could be so violent.  Making her 5'6\\" loses this dramatic point.<br /><br />Second: Facially, in the novels, Lisbeth is not pretty, or would even be considered attractive on any level.  She is described as having a boyish figure and facial piercings.  This is accomplished in the Swedish version with Noomi Rapace -- nude, she is flat chested and her body and face are just so-so.  In bed she is not truly sexy or sensual -- she gets on, gets off, and is finished.  She does not make love.  The Swedish director deliberately cast this actress to accomplish this faithfulness to the novel.  The American director made Rooney Mara look -- at times -- beautiful.  She does not have a boyish body; she has a very young womanly body with normal size breasts. In bed, she is very attractive.  Very sexual, very sensual.  She makes love.  This is not Larsson's Lisbeth.<br /><br />Third: Psychologically, Lisbeth is an introvert who is filled with hostility.  In the Swedish version, the reporter slowly works his way into her confidence, and she (somewhat) lets her guard down for him.  The American version carries none of Lisbeth's borderline psychosis.  Rooney Mara, as Lisbeth, just \\"acts\\" like she is pissed off at the world.  It doesn't take much for the reporter to get her into bed.<br /><br />Conclusion: Stieg Larsson in his triolgy set himself a monumental task:  He asked himself, can I make a small unattractive girl, who is extremely introverted and hostile, and continually acts antisocial and like a bitch -- have the reader (audience) come to like (maybe love) her? This is a girl that we would never sit next to on a crowded bus -- but by the end of the novels (movie) we'd have her over for dinner.  Make no mistake, probably 90% of everyone who saw the Swedish film disliked this obnoxious character from the opening scenes, but by the end of the movie we were rooting for her.  The American version loses this quality of character.  Rooney Mara is tall, attractive, and her Lisbeth is not really super obnoxious or completely introverted.  Once again, her version of the character just \\"acts\\" pissed off.  The director missed the point.<br /><br />Sure, see both versions, and maybe read the books.  If you want Lisbeth Salander, you will only find her in the Swedish version.  The reviewers who thought the American version was as good or better than the Swedish version, did not understand the character of Lisbeth.

081297381X|2.0|As someone most familiar with the mainstream view of things from an academic education, a morbid sense of curiosity and iconoclasm, I'm always interested in well argued challenges to the establishment. This made Taleb's work seem a rather fitting read.<br /><br />Given the many excellent existing reviews here, it would be repetitive to run through well worn ground again. The short take is that there are some interesting ideas mixed in with a health dose of narcissism, and examples meant more to than convey a point than historical accuracy. A decent enough effort, at 3 stars at best in that context.<br /><br />Rather, the point of this bit is to share an brief experience with the author himself. I ran into a small online community he runs, and with the curiosity getting the better part of me, I was motivated enough to read and then write some well thought out short critiques of ideas he was sharing for his new book. To be more specific, they were more of a suggestion to think about an issue of \\"dependence\\" from the viewpoint of dependents as beneficiaries in the relationship instead of only as subjects. The same curiosity also led me to check the day after to see if there was perhaps any reply.<br /><br />Now, I'm not one with enough vanity to expect a response as an equal, but I didn't expect well worded suggestions to be deleted and my ability to ask for the reason to be rescinded. From this it wouldn't be unreasonable to conclude that Mr. Taleb is more interested in surrounding himself in yes-men to prepare his ego for his next work rather than allow any flexibility in his thought process, and I believe this should be relevant to his many fans who take his seeming sense of inerrancy seriously.

0399159029|5.0|After several novels dealing largely with the postwar, Philip Kerr brings Bernie Gunther back to his roots as the last honest cop in Nazi Germany. This installment takes place in fall 1941 and concerns Gunther's peculiar relationship with the feared Reinhard Heydrich, now the National Socialist overlord of the former Czechoslovakia, where he will later be assassinated.<br /><br />Gunther is invited by Heydrich to a social gathering at Heydrich's elegantly appointed villa near Prague. Many top SS men are there. The guests enjoy luxury unknown now in Berlin, where the mobilizing of 3 million men on the Eastern front has put virtually everything in short supply. It's not just a social gathering, though: Heydrich has another agenda.<br /><br />And Gunther is suddenly pressed into service when Heydrich's aide is shot to death. Gunther gets carte blanche to interview everyone there, regardless of their rank o scary reputation, in search of the killer.<br /><br />Gunther's not alone in Prague. He's brought with him a bar girl from Berlin whom he recently saved from being assaulted in an incident with links to two murders and possible espionage overtones. She's smart and sexy and Gunther is falling for her. But as trouble develops in Prague, he realizes she's in danger.<br /><br />The book smacks of an Agatha Christie novel, with the detective interviewing all the house guests, finding much to hide and connections between them going back decades, plus the obligatory consideration of whether the butler did it.<br /><br />But no Agatha Christie novel features both detective and victim tormented by what they've recently seen in the East, where, following the invasion of the Soviet Union, genocide is under way.<br /><br />And Hercule Poirot never had to find a murderer in a country house where nearly everyone already is a murderer.<br /><br />I've enjoyed all the Gunther novels.  Getting back to his roots as an honest cop navigating his career and his principles through the sewer of Nazi corruption is a delight for readers after his more complicated sojourns in Peron's Argentina, Batista's Cuba and Cold War West Germany. This story is right in Kerr's wheelhouse. And he hits it out of the park.er of Nazi corruption is a delight for readers after his more complicated sojourns in Peron's Argentina, Batista's Cuba and Cold War West Germany. This story is right in Kerr's wheelhouse. And he hits it out of the park.

B00007LTIB|5.0|Before you compare this with another 3 song Yes album released 2 years earlier, the only thing this album seems to have in common with Close To The Edge is that it has 3 songs- one epic and 2 shorter pieces. Aside from that, this is almost the \\"evil twin\\" to CTTE (not a putdown, just that the 2 are totally different styles). It came out after the ambitious Tales From Topographic Oceans (4 songs which took up each side of the double LP). Rick Wakeman had left after the tour and so Yes brought in Swiss keyboardist Patrick Moraz, whose style gave Relayer a more jazz feel and the album was supposedly recorded at Chris Squire's house in his basement. Some people say this is Yes's harshest sounding album and it's hard for me to disagree, though much of the harshness especially on the sidelong opening track is quite appropriate. \\"The Gates of Delerium\\" is Jon Anderson at his most direct lyrically, \\"Stand and fight we do consider\\", as he goes from singing softly (\\"Listen...\\") to almost shouting the lyrics when appropriate (\\"...the devil's sermon\\"). The middle section features an extended instrumental section complete with militant marches and sounds of battle, though the mood peacefully changes like a breath of fresh air at the \\"Soon\\" finale. Steve Howe's guitar solo on \\"Sound Chaser\\" is eerie, while Patrick Moraz features some cool spacey jazz fusion on the keyboards. Meanwhile, Chris Squire and Alan White bash it out on the bass and drums, respectively. It ends in a free-for-all jam with Jon singing \\"cha-cha-cha.\\" He took a little crticism for it, but to me, it shows Jon having a little spontaneous fun. \\"To Be Over\\" is a more peaceful but still semi-moody track, melodically reminiscent of Yes' early days, albiet with more modern touches with Moraz's keyboards and Howe's guitar and sitar work. Jon's lyrics are both persuasive (\\"Think it over\\") and spiritual (\\"Be ready to be loved.\\") All in all, this album serves as a new milestone in Yes' ever-changing style (one that wouldn't be repeated, as this was Moraz's only studio LP with Yes).

1439170916|5.0|This book is an extraordinary achievement on many levels.  Besides being beautifully written, it is vast in scope, astoundingly detailed, erudite without being arrogant, and accessible to non-scientific types.  Cancer has touched my family and friends more times than I like to think about, but this book opened my eyes; it not only educated me about the history of cancer, it also revealed to me the exquisite elusiveness of this, the true \\"emperor of all all maladies.\\"  My husband urged me to read it, saying, \\"You're the one who always likes to read about diseases!\\"  This is true.  It seems that the only non-fiction I read has to do with diseases:  influenza, the plague, cholera.  So although I began with some trepidation, I was so pleased to have done so.  Mukherjee weaves history with poignant and sometimes tragic patient stories and cleverly applicable quotations.  This is not just a book written by an oncologist; it is a work of art and philosophy.  It also exposes the cat-and-mouse game often played by researchers and pharmaceutical companies, who care mostly for profit, not curing illness.  I got somewhat bogged down in the last 100 pages or so, amid the discussion of the genetic components of cancer and the sequencing of the cancer genomes, as well as the mutations which must occur for cancer to develop, but I absorbed most of it.  I recently saw an article in a newspaper about genes and cancer and I knew what it was talking about! Most important, for me, is that I gained a much deeper understanding of cancer and a new respect for this, perhaps medicine's most formidable foe.  The thousand year old war continues today, and as Mukherjee says, \\"The question then will not be if we will encounter this immortal illness in our lives, but when.\\"  Memorable piece of work. Highly recommended.
